<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Psychological state of holding a proposition or premise to be true</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the general concept. <p>A <b>belief</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Attitude_(psychology)" title="Attitude (psychology)">attitude</a> that something is the case, or that some <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a> is <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">true</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>, philosophers use the term "belief" to refer to attitudes about the world which can be either <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">true or false</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Belief_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Belief-2">[2]</a></sup> To believe something is to take it to be true; for instance, to believe that snow is white is comparable to accepting the truth of the <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">proposition</a> "snow is white". However, holding a belief does not require active <a href="/wiki/Introspection" title="Introspection">introspection</a>. For example, few carefully consider whether or not the sun will rise tomorrow, simply assuming that it will. Moreover, beliefs need not be <i>occurrent</i> (e.g. a person actively thinking "snow is white"), but can instead be <i>dispositional</i> (e.g. a person who if asked about the color of snow would assert "snow is white").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Belief_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Belief-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are various different ways that contemporary <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosophers</a> have tried to describe beliefs, including as representations of ways that the world could be (<a href="/wiki/Jerry_Fodor" title="Jerry Fodor">Jerry Fodor</a>), as dispositions to act as if certain things are true (<a href="/wiki/Roderick_Chisholm" title="Roderick Chisholm">Roderick Chisholm</a>), as interpretive schemes for making sense of someone's actions (<a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donald_Davidson_(philosopher)" title="Donald Davidson (philosopher)">Donald Davidson</a>), or as mental states that fill a particular function (<a href="/wiki/Hilary_Putnam" title="Hilary Putnam">Hilary Putnam</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Belief_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Belief-2">[2]</a></sup> Some have also attempted to offer significant revisions to our notion of belief, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eliminativism" title="Eliminativism">eliminativists</a> about belief who argue that there is no phenomenon in the natural world which corresponds to our <a href="/wiki/Folk_psychology" title="Folk psychology">folk psychological</a> concept of belief (<a href="/wiki/Paul_Churchland" title="Paul Churchland">Paul Churchland</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Formal_epistemology" title="Formal epistemology">formal epistemologists</a> who aim to replace our bivalent notion of belief ("either we have a belief or we don't have a belief") with the more permissive, probabilistic notion of credence ("there is an entire spectrum of degrees of belief, not a simple dichotomy between belief and non-belief").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Belief_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Belief-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Formal_Belief_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Formal_Belief-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beliefs are the subject of various important philosophical debates. Notable examples include: "What is the rational way to revise one's beliefs when presented with various sorts of evidence?," "Is the content of our beliefs entirely determined by our mental states, or do the relevant facts have any bearing on our beliefs (e.g. if I believe that I'm holding a glass of water, is the non-mental fact that water is H<sub>2</sub>O part of the content of that belief)?," "How fine-grained or coarse-grained are our beliefs?," and "Must it be possible for a belief to be expressible in language, or are there non-linguistic beliefs?".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Belief_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Belief-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conceptions">Conceptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Conceptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Various conceptions of the essential features of beliefs have been proposed, but there is no consensus as to which is the right one. <i>Representationalism</i> is the traditionally dominant position. In its most common form, it holds that beliefs are mental attitudes towards representations, which are usually identified with propositions. These attitudes are part of the internal constitution of the mind holding the attitude. This view contrasts with <i>functionalism</i>, which defines beliefs not in terms of the internal constitution of the mind but in terms of the function or the causal role played by beliefs. According to <i>dispositionalism</i>, beliefs are identified with dispositions to behave in certain ways. This view can be seen as a form of functionalism, defining beliefs in terms of the behavior they tend to cause. <i>Interpretationism</i> constitutes another conception, which has gained popularity in contemporary philosophy. It holds that the beliefs of an entity are in some sense dependent on or relative to someone's interpretation of this entity. <i>Representationalism</i> tends to be associated with a mind-body-dualism. <i>Naturalist</i> considerations against this dualism are among the motivations for choosing one of the alternative conceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Representationalism">Representationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Representationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Representationalism characterizes beliefs in terms of <a href="/wiki/Mental_representation" title="Mental representation">mental representations</a>. Representations are usually defined as objects with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semantic_properties" title="Semantic properties">semantic properties</a>—like having a content, referring to something, or being true or false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitt_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pitt-5">[5]</a></sup> Beliefs form a special class of mental representations since they do not involve sensory qualities in order to represent something, unlike perceptions or episodic memories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert-6">[6]</a></sup> Because of this, it seems natural to construe beliefs as attitudes towards propositions, which also constitute non-sensory representations, i.e. as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propositional_attitudes" title="Propositional attitudes">propositional attitudes</a>. As <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_attitude" title="Mental attitude">mental attitudes</a>, beliefs are characterized by both their content and their mode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert-6">[6]</a></sup> The content of an attitude is what this attitude is directed at: its object. Propositional attitudes are directed at propositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitt_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pitt-5">[5]</a></sup> Beliefs are usually distinguished from other propositional attitudes, like desires, by their mode or the way in which they are directed at propositions. The mode of beliefs has a mind-to-world <a href="/wiki/Direction_of_fit" title="Direction of fit">direction of fit</a>: beliefs try to represent the world as it is; they do not, unlike desires, involve an intention to change it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert-6">[6]</a></sup> For example, if Rahul believes that it will be sunny today, then he has a mental attitude towards the proposition "It will be sunny today" which affirms that this proposition is true. This is different from Sofía's desire that it will be sunny today, despite the fact that both Rahul and Sofía have attitudes toward the same proposition. The mind-to-world direction of fit of beliefs is sometimes expressed by saying that beliefs aim at truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> This aim is also reflected in the tendency to <a href="/wiki/Belief_revision" title="Belief revision">revise one's belief</a> upon receiving new evidence that an existing belief is false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> So, upon hearing a forecast of bad weather, Rahul is likely to change his mental attitude but Sofía is not.
</p><p>There are different ways of conceiving how mental representations are realized in the mind. One form is the <i><a href="/wiki/Language_of_thought_hypothesis" title="Language of thought hypothesis">language of thought hypothesis</a></i>, which claims that mental representations have a language-like structure, sometimes referred to as "mentalese".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-11">[11]</a></sup> 
Just like regular language, this involves simple elements that are combined in various ways according to syntactic rules to form more complex elements that act as bearers of meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-11">[11]</a></sup> On this conception, holding a belief would involve storing such a complex element in one's mind. Different beliefs are separated from each other in that they correspond to different elements stored in the mind. A more holistic alternative to the "language of thought hypothesis" is the <i>map-conception</i>, which uses an analogy to maps to elucidate the nature of beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-11">[11]</a></sup> According to this view, the belief-system of a mind should be conceived of not as a set of many individual sentences but as a map encoding the information contained in these sentences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-11">[11]</a></sup> For example, the fact that Brussels is halfway between Paris and Amsterdam can be expressed both linguistically as a sentence and in a map through its internal geometrical relations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Functionalism">Functionalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Functionalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Functionalism (philosophy of mind)">Functionalism</a> contrasts with representationalism in that it defines beliefs not in terms of the internal constitution of the mind but in terms of the function or the causal role played by them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polger_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polger-13">[13]</a></sup> This view is often combined with the idea that the same belief can be realized in various ways and that it does not matter how it is realized as long as it plays the causal role characteristic to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> As an analogy, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_drive" title="Hard drive">hard drive</a> is defined in a functionalist manner: it performs the function of storing and retrieving digital data. This function can be realized in many different ways: being made of plastic or steel, or using magnetism or laser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> Functionalists hold that something similar is true for beliefs (or mental states in general).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polger_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polger-13">[13]</a></sup> Among the roles relevant for beliefs is their relation to perceptions and to actions: perceptions usually cause beliefs and beliefs cause actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> For example, seeing that a traffic light has switched to red is usually associated with a belief that the light is red, which in turn causes the driver to bring the car to a halt. Functionalists use such characteristics to define beliefs: whatever is caused by perceptions in a certain way and also causes behavior in a certain way is called a belief. This is not just true for humans but may include animals, hypothetical aliens or even computers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Levin-12">[12]</a></sup> From this perspective, it would make sense to ascribe the belief that a traffic light is red to a <a href="/wiki/Self-driving_car" title="Self-driving car">self-driving car</a> behaving just like a human driver.
</p><p>Dispositionalism is sometimes seen as a specific form of functionalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> It defines beliefs only concerning their role as causes of behavior or as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dispositions" title="Dispositions">dispositions</a> to behave in a certain way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quilty-Dunn_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quilty-Dunn-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Audi_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-16">[16]</a></sup> For example, a belief that there is a pie in the pantry is associated with the disposition to affirm this when asked and to go to the pantry when hungry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert-6">[6]</a></sup> While it is uncontroversial that beliefs shape our behavior, the thesis that beliefs can be defined exclusively through their role in producing behavior has been contested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quilty-Dunn_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quilty-Dunn-15">[15]</a></sup> The problem arises because the mechanisms shaping our behavior seem to be too complex to single out the general contribution of one particular belief for any possible situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert-6">[6]</a></sup> For example, one may decide not to affirm that there is a pie in the pantry when asked, because one wants to keep it secret. Or one might not eat the pie despite being hungry, because one also believes that it is poisoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borchert_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Borchert-6">[6]</a></sup> Due to this complexity, we are unable to define even a belief as simple as this one in terms of the behavioral dispositions for which it could be responsible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpretationism">Interpretationism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Interpretationism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to interpretationism, the beliefs of an entity are in some sense dependent on or relative to someone's interpretation of this entity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Child1_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Child1-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> is an important defender of such a position. He holds that we ascribe beliefs to entities in order to predict how they will behave. Entities with simple behavioral patterns can be described using physical laws or in terms of their function. Dennett refers to these forms of explanation as the "physical stance" and the "design stance". These stances are contrasted with the <a href="/wiki/Intentional_stance" title="Intentional stance">intentional stance</a>, which is applied to entities with a more complex behavior by ascribing beliefs and desires to these entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> For example, we can predict that a chess player will move her queen to f7 if we ascribe to her the desire to win the game and the belief that this move will achieve that. The same procedure can also be applied to predicting how a chess computer will behave. The entity has the belief in question if this belief can be used to predict its behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> Having a belief is relative to an interpretation since there may be different equally good ways of ascribing beliefs to predict behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-16"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> So there may be another interpretation that predicts the move of the queen to f7 that does not involve the belief that this move will win the game. Another version of interpretationism is due to <a href="/wiki/Donald_Davidson_(philosopher)" title="Donald Davidson (philosopher)">Donald Davidson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Child1_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Child1-17">[17]</a></sup> who uses the thought experiment of <a href="/wiki/Radical_interpretation" title="Radical interpretation">radical interpretation</a>, in which the goal is to make sense of the behavior and language of another person from scratch without any knowledge of this person's language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-17"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> This process involves ascribing beliefs and desires to the speaker. The speaker really has these beliefs if this project can be successful in principle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-18"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Interpretationism can be combined with eliminativism and instrumentalism about beliefs. Eliminativists hold that, strictly speaking, there are no beliefs. Instrumentalists agree with eliminativists but add that belief-ascriptions are useful nonetheless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-19"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> This usefulness can be explained in terms of interpretationism: belief-ascriptions help us in predicting how entities will behave. It has been argued that interpretationism can also be understood in a more realistic sense: that entities really have the beliefs ascribed to them and that these beliefs participate in the causal network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Child4_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Child4-20">[20]</a></sup> But, for this to be possible, it may be necessary to define interpretationism as a methodology and not as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological" title="Ontological">ontological</a> outlook on beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Child1_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Child1-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical">Historical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Historical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the context of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient Greek thought</a>, three related concepts were identified regarding the concept of belief: <i><a href="/wiki/Pistis#Pistis_in_rhetoric" title="Pistis">pistis</a>,</i> <i><a href="/wiki/Doxa" title="Doxa">doxa</a>,</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogma</a>.</i> Simplified, <i>pistis</i> refers to "<a href="/wiki/Trust_(social_science)" title="Trust (social science)">trust</a>" and "confidence," <i>doxa</i> refers to "<a href="/wiki/Opinion" title="Opinion">opinion</a>" and "acceptance," and <i>dogma</i> refers to the <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_theory" title="Philosophical theory">positions</a> of a philosopher or of a <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_movement" title="Philosophical movement">philosophical school</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Beliefs can be categorized into various types depending on their ontological status, their degree, their object or their semantic properties.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrent_and_dispositional">Occurrent and dispositional</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Occurrent and dispositional">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Having an occurrent belief that the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a> is in Arizona involves entertaining the representation associated with this belief—for example, by actively thinking about it. But the great majority of our beliefs are not active most of the time: they are merely dispositional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-20"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> They usually become activated or occurrent when needed or relevant in some way and then fall back into their dispositional state afterward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-21"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> For example, the belief that 57 is greater than 14 was probably dispositional to the reader before reading this sentence, has become occurrent while reading it and may soon become dispositional again as the mind focuses elsewhere. The distinction between occurrent and dispositional beliefs is sometimes identified with the distinction between conscious and unconscious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartlett_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frise_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frise-22">[22]</a></sup> But it has been argued that, despite overlapping, the two distinctions do not match. The reason for this is that beliefs can shape one's behavior and be involved in one's reasoning even if the subject is not conscious of them. Such beliefs are cases of unconscious occurrent mental states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartlett_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett-21">[21]</a></sup> On this view, being occurrent corresponds to being active, either consciously or unconsciously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frise_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frise-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>A dispositional belief is not the same as a disposition to believe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Audi_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-16">[16]</a></sup> We have various dispositions to believe given the right perceptions; for example, to believe that it is raining given a perception of rain. Without this perception, there is still a disposition to believe but no actual dispositional belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Audi_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-16">[16]</a></sup> On a dispositionalist conception of belief, there are no occurrent beliefs, since all beliefs are defined in terms of dispositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-22"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Full_and_partial">Full and partial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Full and partial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An important dispute in formal epistemology concerns the question of whether beliefs should be conceptualized as <i>full</i> beliefs or as <i>partial</i> beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genin_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Genin-23">[23]</a></sup> Full beliefs are all-or-nothing attitudes: either one has a belief in a proposition or one does not. This conception is sufficient to understand many belief ascriptions found in everyday language: for example, Pedro's belief that the Earth is bigger than the Moon. But some cases involving comparisons between beliefs are not easily captured through full beliefs alone: for example, that Pedro's belief that the Earth is bigger than the Moon is more certain than his belief that the Earth is bigger than Venus. Such cases are most naturally analyzed in terms of partial beliefs involving degrees of belief, so-called <i>credences</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genin_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Genin-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson-24">[24]</a></sup> The higher the degree of a belief, the more certain the believer is that the believed proposition is true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartmann_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartmann-25">[25]</a></sup> This is usually formalized by numbers between 0 and 1: a degree of 1 represents an absolutely certain belief, a belief of 0 corresponds to an absolutely certain disbelief and all the numbers in between correspond to intermediate degrees of certainty. In the <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_epistemology" title="Bayesian epistemology">Bayesian approach</a>, these degrees are interpreted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subjective_probabilities" title="Subjective probabilities">subjective probabilities</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pettigrew_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pettigrew-27">[27]</a></sup> e.g. a belief of degree 0.9 that it will rain tomorrow means that the agent thinks that the probability of rain tomorrow is 90%. Bayesianism uses this relation between beliefs and probability to define the norms of rationality in terms of the laws of probability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartmann_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hartmann-25">[25]</a></sup> This includes both synchronic laws about what one should believe at any moment and diachronic laws about how one should revise one's beliefs upon receiving new evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartmann_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hartmann-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The central question in the dispute between full and partial beliefs is whether these two types are really distinct types or whether one type can be explained in terms of the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genin_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Genin-23">[23]</a></sup> One answer to this question is called the <i>Lockean thesis</i>. It states that partial beliefs are basic and that full beliefs are to be conceived as partial beliefs above a certain threshold: for example, that every belief above 0.9 is a full belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genin_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Genin-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Defenders of a primitive notion of full belief, on the other hand, have tried to explain partial beliefs as full beliefs about probabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genin_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-Genin-23">[23]</a></sup> On this view, having a partial belief of degree 0.9 that it will rain tomorrow is the same as having a full belief that the probability of rain tomorrow is 90%. Another approach circumvents the notion of probability altogether and replaces degrees of belief with degrees of disposition to revise one's full belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genin_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-Genin-23">[23]</a></sup> From this perspective, both a belief of degree 0.6 and a belief of degree 0.9 may be seen as full beliefs. The difference between them is that the former belief can readily be changed upon receiving new evidence while the latter is more stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genin_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-Genin-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief-in_and_belief-that">Belief-in and belief-that</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Belief-in and belief-that">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traditionally, philosophers have mainly focused in their inquiries concerning belief on the notion of <i>belief-that</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price-30">[30]</a></sup> Belief-that can be characterized as a <a href="/wiki/Propositional_attitude" title="Propositional attitude">propositional attitude</a> to a claim which is either true or false. <i>Belief-in</i>, on the other hand, is more closely related to notions like trust or faith in that it refers usually to an attitude to persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price-30">[30]</a></sup> <i>Belief-in</i> plays a central role in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_traditions" title="Religious traditions">religious traditions</a> in which <i>belief in God</i> is one of the central virtues of their followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-31">[31]</a></sup> The difference between belief-in and belief-that is sometimes blurry since various expressions using the term "belief in" seem to be translatable into corresponding expressions using the term "belief that" instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macintosh2_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macintosh2-32">[32]</a></sup> For example, a <i>belief in</i> fairies may be said to be a <i>belief that</i> fairies exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-31">[31]</a></sup> In this sense, belief-in is often used when the entity is not real, or its existence is in doubt. Typical examples would include: "he believes in witches and ghosts" or "many children believe in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a>" or "I believe in a deity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macintosh_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macintosh-33">[33]</a></sup> Not all usages of belief-in concern the existence of something: some are <i>commendatory</i> in that they express a positive attitude towards their object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macintosh_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Macintosh-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Price-30">[30]</a></sup> It has been suggested that these cases can also be accounted for in terms of belief-that. For example, a <i>belief in</i> marriage could be translated as a <i>belief that</i> marriage is good.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-31">[31]</a></sup> Belief-in is used in a similar sense when expressing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-confidence" title="Self-confidence">self-confidence</a> or faith in one's self or one's abilities.
</p><p>Defenders of a reductive account of belief-in have used this line of thought to argue that <i>belief in God</i> can be analyzed in a similar way: e.g. that it amounts to a belief that God exists with his characteristic attributes, like <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a> and <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-31">[31]</a></sup> Opponents of this account often concede that belief-in may entail various forms of belief-that, but that there are additional aspects to belief-in that are not reducible to belief-that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macintosh2_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Macintosh2-32">[32]</a></sup> For example, a <i>belief in</i> an ideal may involve the <i>belief that</i> this ideal is something good, but it additionally involves a positive evaluative attitude toward this ideal that goes beyond a mere propositional attitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-31">[31]</a></sup> Applied to the <i>belief in</i> God, opponents of the reductive approach may hold that a <i>belief that</i> God exists may be a necessary pre-condition for <i>belief in</i> God, but that it is not sufficient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macintosh2_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Macintosh2-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="De_dicto_and_de_re">De dicto and de re</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: De dicto and de re">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The difference between <i><a href="/wiki/De_dicto_and_de_re" title="De dicto and de re">de dicto and de re</a></i> beliefs or the corresponding ascriptions concerns the contributions singular terms like names and other referential devices make to the semantic properties of the belief or its ascription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-23"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broackes_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broackes-34">[34]</a></sup> In regular contexts, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Truth-value" title="Truth-value">truth-value</a> of a sentence does not change upon substitution of co-referring terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-35">[35]</a></sup> For example, since the names "Superman" and "Clark Kent" refer to the same person, we can replace one with the other in the sentence "Superman is strong" without changing its truth-value. But this issue is more complicated in case of belief ascriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-35">[35]</a></sup> For example, Lois believes that Superman is strong but she does not believe that Clark Kent is strong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-24"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup> This difficulty arises due to the fact that she does not know that the two names refer to the same entity. Beliefs or belief ascriptions for which this substitution does not generally work are <i>de dicto</i>, otherwise, they are <i>de re</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-25"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broackes_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Broackes-34">[34]</a></sup> So, in a de re sense, Lois does believe that Clark Kent is strong, while in a de dicto sense she does not. The contexts corresponding to de dicto ascriptions are known as <a href="/wiki/Opaque_context" title="Opaque context">referentially opaque contexts</a> while de re ascriptions are <i>referentially transparent</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-26"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collective_belief">Collective belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Collective belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A collective belief is referred to when people speak of what "we" believe when this is not simply elliptical for what "we all" believe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">Sociologist</a> <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a> wrote of collective beliefs and proposed that they, like all "<a href="/wiki/Social_fact" title="Social fact">social facts</a>", "inhered in" social <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_(sociology)" title="Group (sociology)">groups</a> as opposed to individual persons. <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Dancy" title="Jonathan Dancy">Jonathan Dancy</a> states that "Durkheim's discussion of collective belief, though suggestive, is relatively obscure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Gilbert" title="Margaret Gilbert">Margaret Gilbert</a> has offered a related account in terms of the joint commitment of a number of persons as a body to accept a certain belief. According to this account, individuals who together collectively believe something need not personally believe it individually. Gilbert's work on the topic has stimulated a developing literature among philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> One question that has arisen is whether and how philosophical accounts of belief in general need to be sensitive to the possibility of collective belief.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief_contents">Belief contents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Belief contents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Mental_representation" title="Mental representation">mental representations</a>, beliefs have <i>contents</i>. The content of a belief is what this belief is about or what it represents. Within philosophy, there are various disputes about how the contents of beliefs are to be understood. Holists and molecularists hold that the content of one particular belief depends on or is determined by other beliefs belonging to the same subject, which is denied by atomists. The question of dependence or determination also plays a central role in the internalism-externalism-debate. Internalism states that the contents of someone's beliefs depend only on what is internal to that person: they are determined entirely by things going on inside this person's head. Externalism, on the other hand, holds that the relations to one's environment also have a role to play in this.
</p>
<h3><span id="Atomism.2C_molecularism_and_holism"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomism,_molecularism_and_holism">Atomism, molecularism and holism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Atomism, molecularism and holism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The disagreement between <i>atomism, molecularism and holism</i> concerns the question of how the content of one belief depends on the contents of other beliefs held by the same subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Block-39">[39]</a></sup> Atomists deny such dependence relations, molecularists restrict them to only a few closely related beliefs while holists hold that they may obtain between any two beliefs, however unrelated they seem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-27"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitt_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pitt-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Block-39">[39]</a></sup> For example, assume that Mei and Benjamin both affirm that <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> is a planet. The most straightforward explanation, given by the atomists, would be that they have the same belief, i.e. that they hold the same content to be true. But now assume that Mei also believes that <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> is a planet, which is denied by Benjamin. This indicates that they have different concepts of <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>, which would mean that they were affirming different contents when they both agreed that Jupiter is a planet. This reasoning leads to molecularism or holism because the content of the Jupiter-belief depends on the Pluto-belief in this example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-28"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Block-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>An important motivation for this position comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._V._Quine" title="W. V. Quine">W. V. Quine</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confirmational_holism" title="Confirmational holism">confirmational holism</a>, which holds that, because of this interconnectedness, we cannot confirm or disconfirm individual hypotheses, that confirmation happens on the level of the theory as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-Block-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Another motivation is due to considerations of the nature of learning: it is often not possible to understand one concept, like <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtonian_physics" title="Newtonian physics">Newtonian physics</a>, without understanding other concepts, like <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> or <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-Block-39">[39]</a></sup> One problem for holism is that genuine disagreements seem to be impossible or very rare: disputants would usually talk past each other since they never share exactly the same web of beliefs needed to determine the content of the source of the disagreement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-29"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block_39-5"><a href="#cite_note-Block-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internalism_and_externalism">Internalism and externalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Internalism and externalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i>Internalism and externalism</i> disagree about whether the contents of our beliefs are determined only by what's happening in our head or also by other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-30"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitt_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pitt-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowlands_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rowlands-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-42">[42]</a></sup> Internalists deny such a dependence on external factors. They hold that a person and a molecule-by-molecule copy would have exactly the same beliefs. <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Putnam" title="Hilary Putnam">Hilary Putnam</a> objects to this position by way of his <a href="/wiki/Twin_Earth_thought_experiment" title="Twin Earth thought experiment">twin Earth thought experiment</a>. He imagines a twin Earth in another part of the universe that is exactly like ours, except that their water has a different chemical composition despite behaving just like ours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-31"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowlands_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rowlands-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-42">[42]</a></sup> According to Putnam, the reader's thought that water is wet is about <i>our water</i> while the reader's twin's thought on twin Earth that water is wet is about <i>their water</i>. This is the case despite the fact that the two readers have the same molecular composition. So it seems necessary to include external factors in order to explain the difference. One problem with this position is that this difference in content does not bring any causal difference with it: the two readers act in exactly the same way. This casts doubt on the thesis that there is any genuine difference in need of explanation between the contents of the two beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwitzgebel_4-32"><a href="#cite_note-Schwitzgebel-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowlands_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rowlands-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:238px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="342" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/236px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/354px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/472px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png 2x" width="236"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn diagram</a> illustrating the traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definition_of_knowledge" title="Definition of knowledge">definition of knowledge</a> as justified true belief (represented by the yellow circle). The Gettier problem gives us reason to think that not all justified true beliefs constitute knowledge.</div></div></div>
<p>Epistemology is concerned with delineating the boundary between justified belief and <a href="/wiki/Opinion" title="Opinion">opinion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and involved generally with a theoretical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophical" title="Philosophical">philosophical</a> study of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>. The primary problem in epistemology is to understand what is needed to have knowledge. In a notion derived from <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s dialogue <i><a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theaetetus</a></i>, where the epistemology of <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> most clearly departs from that of the <a href="/wiki/Sophist" title="Sophist">sophists</a>, who appear to have defined knowledge as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>". The tendency to base knowledge (<i><a href="/wiki/Episteme" title="Episteme">episteme</a></i>) on common opinion (<i><a href="/wiki/Doxa" title="Doxa">doxa</a></i>) Socrates dismisses, results from failing to distinguish a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dispositive" title="Dispositive">dispositive</a> belief (<i>doxa</i>) from knowledge (<i>episteme</i>) when the opinion is regarded correct (n.b., <i>orthé</i> not <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alethia" title="Alethia">alethia</a></i>), in terms of right, and juristically so (according to the premises of the dialogue), which was the task of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhetor" title="Rhetor">rhetors</a> to prove. Plato dismisses this possibility of an affirmative relation between opinion and knowledge even when the one who opines grounds his belief on the rule, and is able to add justification (<i><a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">logos</a></i>: reasonable and necessarily plausible assertions/evidence/guidance) to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> A belief can be based fully or partially on <a href="/wiki/Intuition" title="Intuition">intuition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato has been credited for the <i>justified true belief</i> theory of knowledge, even though Plato in the <i>Theaetetus</i> elegantly dismisses it, and even posits this argument of Socrates as a cause for his death penalty. The epistemologists, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Gettier" title="Edmund Gettier">Gettier</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Goldman" title="Alvin Goldman">Goldman</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> have questioned the "justified true belief" definition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justified_true_belief">Justified true belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Justified true belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_knowledge#Justified_true_belief" title="Definitions of knowledge">Definitions of knowledge § Justified true belief</a></div>
<p><b>Justified true belief</b> is a definition of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> that gained approval during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, "justified" standing in contrast to "revealed". There have been attempts to trace it back to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and his dialogues, more specifically in the <i><a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theaetetus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Meno" title="Meno">Meno</a></i>. The concept of justified true belief states that in order to know that a given proposition is true, one must not only believe the relevant true proposition, but also have justification for doing so. In more formal terms, an agent <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4611d85173cd3b508e67077d4a1252c9c05abca2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.499ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> knows that a proposition <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is true <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">if and only if</a>:
</p>
<ul><li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is true, and</li>
<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>S</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4611d85173cd3b508e67077d4a1252c9c05abca2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.499ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is justified in believing that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is true</li></ul>
<p>This theory of knowledge suffered a significant setback with the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problem" title="Gettier problem">Gettier problems</a>, situations in which the above conditions were seemingly met but where many philosophers deny that anything is known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nozick" title="Robert Nozick">Robert Nozick</a> suggested a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gettier_problems#Fred_Dretske's_conclusive_reasons_and_Robert_Nozick's_truth-tracking" title="Gettier problems">clarification</a> of "justification" which he believed eliminates the problem: the justification has to be such that were the justification false, the knowledge would be false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Bernecker and Dretske (2000) argue that "no epistemologist since Gettier has seriously and successfully defended the traditional view."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup> On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Boghossian" title="Paul Boghossian">Paul Boghossian</a> argues that the justified true belief account is the "standard, widely accepted" definition of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boghossian_on_Justification_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boghossian_on_Justification-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief_systems">Belief systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Belief systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <b>belief system</b> comprises a set of mutually supportive beliefs. The beliefs of any such system can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_belief" title="Religious belief">religious</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideological</a>, or a combination of these.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meadows2008_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-meadows2008-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Glover.27s_view"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Glover's_view">Glover's view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Glover's view">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Glover" title="Jonathan Glover">Jonathan Glover</a>, following Meadows (2008), says that beliefs are always part of a belief system, and that tenanted belief systems are difficult for the tenants to completely revise or reject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> He suggests that beliefs have to be considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holistically" title="Holistically">holistically</a>, and that no belief exists in isolation in the mind of the believer. Each belief always implicates and relates to other beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-54">[54]</a></sup> Glover provides the example of a patient with an illness who returns to a doctor, but the doctor says that the prescribed medicine is not working. At that point, the patient has a great deal of flexibility in choosing what beliefs to keep or reject: the patient could believe that the doctor is incompetent, that the doctor's assistants made a mistake, that the patient's own body is unique in some unexpected way, that Western medicine is ineffective, or even that Western science is entirely unable to discover truths about ailments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>This insight has relevance for <a href="/wiki/Inquisitor" title="Inquisitor">inquisitors</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missionaries" title="Missionaries">missionaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agitprop" title="Agitprop">agitprop</a> groups and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/thought_police" title="wiktionary:thought police">thought-police</a>. The British philosopher <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Law" title="Stephen Law">Stephen Law</a> has described some belief systems (including belief in <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychic_powers" title="Psychic powers">psychic powers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alien_abduction" title="Alien abduction">alien abduction</a>) as <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/claptrap" title="wikt:claptrap">"claptrap"</a> and says that such belief-systems can "draw people in and hold them captive so they become willing slaves of claptrap ... if you get sucked in, it can be extremely difficult to think your way clear again".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
