<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Religion_collage_updated.jpg"><img alt="Religion collage updated.jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1382" data-file-width="2017" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/220px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/330px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/440px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Religion_collage_updated.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Religious belief refers to attitudes towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythological" title="Mythological">mythological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> aspects of a <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nye_2008_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nye_2008-57">[57]</a></sup> Religious belief is distinct from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_ritual" title="Religious ritual">religious practice</a> and from <a href="/wiki/Religious_behaviour" title="Religious behaviour">religious behaviours</a> – with some believers not practicing <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> and some practitioners not believing religion. Religious beliefs, deriving from ideas that are exclusive to religion,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> often relate to the existence, characteristics and worship of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> or deities, to the idea of <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">divine intervention</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Personal_life" title="Personal life">human life</a>, or to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deontological" title="Deontological">deontological</a> explanations for the values and practices centered on the teachings of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiritual_leader" title="Spiritual leader">spiritual leader</a> or <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nye_2008_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nye_2008-57">[57]</a></sup> In contrast to other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belief_system" title="Belief system">belief systems</a>, religious beliefs are usually <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codified</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms">Forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A popular view holds that different religions each have identifiable and exclusive sets of beliefs or <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creeds</a>, but surveys of religious belief have often found that the official doctrine and descriptions of the beliefs offered by religious authorities do not always agree with the privately held beliefs of those who identify as members of a particular religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> For a broad classification of the kinds of religious belief, see below.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamentalism">Fundamentalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fundamentalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_fundamentalism" title="Religious fundamentalism">Religious fundamentalism</a></div>
<p>First self-applied as a term to the conservative doctrine outlined by anti-modernist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestants" title="Protestants">Protestants</a> in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A.C._Dixon_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-A.C._Dixon-60">[60]</a></sup> "fundamentalism" in religious terms denotes strict adherence to an interpretation of scriptures that are generally associated with theologically conservative positions or traditional understandings of the text and are distrustful of innovative readings, new revelation, or alternative interpretations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Religious fundamentalism has been identified<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> in the media as being associated with <a href="/wiki/Fanaticism" title="Fanaticism">fanatical</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zealotry" title="Zealotry">zealous</a> political movements around the world that have used a strict adherence to a particular religious doctrine as a means to establish political identity and to enforce societal norms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthodoxy">Orthodoxy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Orthodoxy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">Orthodoxy</a></div>
<p>First used in the context of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a>, the term "orthodoxy" relates to religious belief that closely follows the edicts, <a href="/wiki/Apologetics" title="Apologetics">apologies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics" title="Hermeneutics">hermeneutics</a> of a prevailing religious authority. In the case of Early Christianity, this authority was the communion of bishops, and is often referred to by the term "<a href="/wiki/Magisterium" title="Magisterium">Magisterium</a>". The term <i>orthodox</i> was applied<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2017)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> almost as an epithet to a group of Jewish believers who held to pre-Enlightenment understanding of Judaism – now known as <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> of Christianity and the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> each consider themselves to be the true heir to Early Christian belief and practice. The antonym of "orthodox" is "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterodox" title="Heterodox">heterodox</a>", and those adhering to orthodoxy often accuse the heterodox of <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schism_(religion)" title="Schism (religion)">schism</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>.
</p>
<h4><span id="Modernism.2Freform"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modernism/reform">Modernism/reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Modernism/reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> in Europe exhibited varying degrees of <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a> and intolerance towards new and old religious ideas. The <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophes" title="Philosophes">philosophes</a></i> took particular exception to many of the more fantastical claims of religions and directly challenged religious authority and the prevailing beliefs associated with the established churches. In response to the liberalizing political and social movements, some religious groups attempted to integrate Enlightenment ideals of rationality, equality, and individual liberty into their belief systems, especially in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">Liberal Christianity</a> offer two examples of such religious associations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitudes_to_other_religions">Attitudes to other religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Attitudes to other religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adherents of particular religions deal with the differing doctrines and practices espoused by other religions or by other <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">religious denominations</a> in a variety of ways.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exclusivism">Exclusivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Exclusivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religious_exclusivism" title="Religious exclusivism">Religious exclusivism</a></div>
<p>People with exclusivist beliefs typically explain other beliefs either as in error, or as corruptions or counterfeits of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_true_faith" title="One true faith">true faith</a>. This approach is a fairly consistent feature among smaller <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">new religious movements</a> that often rely on doctrine that claims a unique <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Founders_of_religious_traditions" title="Founders of religious traditions">founders</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leader" title="Leader">leaders</a>, and considers it a matter of faith that the "correct" religion has a monopoly on truth. All three major <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic monotheistic religions</a> have passages in their holy scriptures that attest to the primacy of the scriptural testimony, and indeed <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> itself is often<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (June 2018)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> vouched<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> as an innovation characterized specifically by its explicit rejection of earlier polytheistic faiths.
</p><p>Some exclusivist faiths incorporate a specific element of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proselytization" title="Proselytization">proselytization</a>. This is a strongly-held belief in the Christian tradition which follows the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">Great Commission</a>, and is less emphasized by the Islamic faith where the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quranic</a> edict "There shall be no compulsion in religion" (2:256) is often quoted as a justification for toleration of alternative beliefs. The <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">Jewish tradition</a> does not actively seek out converts.
</p><p>Exclusivism correlates with conservative, fundamentalist, and orthodox approaches of many religions, while pluralistic and syncretist approaches either explicitly downplay or reject the exclusivist tendencies within a religion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inclusivism">Inclusivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Inclusivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>People with <a href="/wiki/Inclusivism" title="Inclusivism">inclusivist</a> beliefs recognize some truth in all faith <a href="/wiki/System" title="System">systems</a>, highlighting agreements and minimizing differences. This attitude is sometimes associated<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interfaith_dialog" title="Interfaith dialog">Interfaith dialogue</a> or with the Christian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecumenical" title="Ecumenical">Ecumenical</a> movement, though in principle such attempts at pluralism are not necessarily inclusivist and many actors in such interactions (for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>) still hold to exclusivist dogma while participating in inter-religious organizations. Explicitly inclusivist religions include many that are associated with the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement, as well as modern reinterpretations of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Baháʼí Faith">Baháʼí Faith</a> considers it doctrine that there is truth in all faith-systems.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">Pluralism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> are two closely related concepts. People with pluralist beliefs make no distinction between faith systems, viewing each one as valid within a particular culture. People with syncretic views blend the views of a variety of different religions or traditional beliefs into a unique fusion which suits their particular <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experiences</a> and contexts (<i>see</i> <a href="/wiki/Eclecticism" title="Eclecticism">eclecticism</a>). <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a> exemplifies a syncretic faith.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adherence">Adherence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Adherence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God</a></div>
<p>Typical reasons for adherence to religion include the following:
</p>
<ul><li>Some see belief in a deity as necessary for <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral behavior</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moore_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-moore-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Some regard religious practices as serene, beautiful, and conducive to religious experiences, which in turn support religious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>Organized religions promote a <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_community" title="Sense of community">sense of community</a> among their followers, and the moral and cultural common ground of these communities makes them attractive to people with similar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Values" title="Values">values</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-repa_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-repa-68">[68]</a></sup> Indeed, while religious beliefs and practices are usually connected, some individuals with substantially secular beliefs still participate in religious practices for cultural reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li>Each religion asserts that it is a means by which its adherents may come into closer contact with the Divine, with Truth, and with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Spiritual_power&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Spiritual power (page does not exist)">spiritual power</a>. They all promise to free adherents from spiritual bondage, and to bring them into spiritual freedom. It naturally follows that a religion which can free its adherents from deception, sin, and spiritual death will have significant mental-health benefits. <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Maslow" title="Abraham Maslow">Abraham Maslow</a>'s research after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> showed that <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">Holocaust</a> survivors tended to be those who held strong religious beliefs (not necessarily temple attendance, etc.), suggesting that belief helped people cope in extreme circumstances. <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_psychology" title="Humanistic psychology">Humanistic psychology</a> went on to investigate how religious or spiritual identity may have correlations with longer lifespan and better health. The study found that humans may particularly need religious ideas to serve various emotional needs such as the need to feel loved, the need to belong to homogeneous groups, the need for understandable explanations and the need for a guarantee of ultimate justice. Other factors may involve sense of <a href="/wiki/Motivation" title="Motivation">purpose</a>, sense of <a href="/wiki/Identity_(social_science)" title="Identity (social science)">identity</a>, or a sense of contact with the divine. See also <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27s_Search_for_Meaning" title="Man's Search for Meaning">Man's Search for Meaning</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Viktor_Frankl" title="Viktor Frankl">Viktor Frankl</a>, detailing his experience with the importance of religion in surviving the Holocaust. Critics assert that the very fact that religion was the primary selector for research subjects may have introduced a bias, and that the fact that all subjects were Holocaust survivors may also have had an effect. According to Larson et al. (2000), "[m]ore longitudinal research with better multidimensional measures will help further clarify the roles of these [religious] factors and whether they are beneficial or harmful."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larson_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-larson-70">[70]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Psychologist <a href="/wiki/James_Alcock" title="James Alcock">James Alcock</a> also summarizes a number of apparent benefits which reinforce religious belief. These include prayer appearing to account for successful resolution of problems, "a bulwark against existential anxiety and fear of annihilation," an increased sense of control, companionship with one's deity, a source of self-significance, and group identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apostasy">Apostasy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Apostasy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">Apostasy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God#Arguments_against_belief_in_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God § Arguments against belief in God</a></div>
<p>Typical reasons for rejection of religion include:
</p>
<ul><li>Some people regard certain fundamental doctrines of some religions as illogical, contrary to experience, or unsupported by sufficient <a href="/wiki/Evidence" title="Evidence">evidence</a>; such people may reject one or more religions for those reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell-72">[72]</a></sup> Even some believers may have difficulty accepting particular religious assertions or doctrines. Some people believe the body of evidence available to humans to be insufficient to justify certain religious beliefs. They may thus disagree with religious interpretations of ethics and human purpose, or with various <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">creation myths</a>. This reason has perhaps<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (November 2017)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup> been aggravated by the protestations and emphases of some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamentalist_Christianity" title="Fundamentalist Christianity">fundamentalist Christians</a>.</li>
<li>Some religions include beliefs that certain groups of people are inferior or sinful and deserve contempt, persecution, or even death, and that non-believers will be punished for their unbelief in an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/After-life" title="After-life">after-life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Adherents to a religion may feel antipathy to unbelievers. Numerous examples exist of people of one religion or sect using religion as an excuse to murder people with different religious beliefs. To mention just a few examples:
<ul><li>the slaughter of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenots</a> by French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholics" title="Catholics">Catholics</a> in the sixteenth century</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> killing each other when <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> separated from India in 1947</li>
<li>the persecution and killing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiite" title="Shiite">Shiite</a> Muslims by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> Muslims in Iraq</li>
<li>the murder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestants</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholics" title="Catholics">Catholics</a> and vice versa in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> (both of these examples in the late twentieth century)</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a> that continues as of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> – According to some critics of religion, such beliefs can encourage completely unnecessary conflicts and in some cases even wars. Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist" title="Atheist">atheists</a> believe that, because of this, religion is incompatible with world peace, freedom, civil rights, equality, and good government. On the other hand, most religions perceive atheism as a threat and will vigorously and even violently<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-74">[74]</a></sup> defend themselves against religious sterilization, making the attempt to remove public religious practices a source of strife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-74">[74]</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>Some people may be unable to accept the values that a specific religion promotes and will therefore not join that religion. They may also be unable to accept the proposition that those who do not believe will go to hell or be damned, especially if said nonbelievers are close to the person.</li>
<li>The maintenance of <a href="/wiki/Personal_life" title="Personal life">life</a> and the achievement of <a href="/wiki/Self-esteem" title="Self-esteem">self-esteem</a> require of a person the fullest exercise of reason<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>—but morality (people are taught<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2017)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup>) rests on and requires faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Belief%22" rel="nofollow">"Belief"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Belief%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Belief%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Belief%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Belief%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Belief%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Mainstream psychology and related disciplines have traditionally treated belief as if it were the simplest form of mental representation and therefore one of the building blocks of conscious thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Philosophers have tended to be more abstract in their analysis, and much of the work examining the viability of the belief concept stems from philosophical analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of belief presumes a subject (the believer) and an object of belief (the proposition). So, like other <a href="/wiki/Propositional_attitude" title="Propositional attitude">propositional attitudes</a>, belief implies the existence of <a href="/wiki/Mental_state" title="Mental state">mental states</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a>, both of which are hotly debated topics in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, whose foundations and relation to brain states are still controversial.
</p><p>Beliefs are sometimes divided into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_beliefs" title="Core beliefs">core beliefs</a> (that are actively thought about) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dispositional_belief" title="Dispositional belief">dispositional beliefs</a> (that may be ascribed to someone who has not thought about the issue). For example, if asked "do you believe tigers wear pink pajamas?" a person might answer that they do not, despite the fact they may never have thought about this situation before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>This has important implications for understanding the <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychology" title="Neuropsychology">neuropsychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> of belief. If the concept of belief is incoherent, then any attempt to find the underlying neural processes that support it will fail.
</p><p>Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Lynne_Rudder_Baker" title="Lynne Rudder Baker">Lynne Rudder Baker</a> has outlined four main contemporary approaches to belief in her book <i>Saving Belief</i>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is correct – Sometimes called the "mental sentence theory," in this conception, beliefs exist as coherent entities, and the way we talk about them in everyday life is a valid basis for scientific endeavor. <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Fodor" title="Jerry Fodor">Jerry Fodor</a> was one of the principal defenders of this point of view.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief may not be entirely correct, but it is close enough to make some useful predictions – This view argues that we will eventually reject the idea of belief as we know it now, but that there may be a correlation between what we take to be a belief when someone says "I believe that snow is white" and how a future theory of psychology will explain this behavior. Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Stich" title="Stephen Stich">Stephen Stich</a> has argued for this particular understanding of belief.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is entirely wrong and will be completely superseded by a radically different theory that will have no use for the concept of belief as we know it – Known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eliminativism" title="Eliminativism">eliminativism</a>, this view (most notably proposed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Churchland" title="Paul Churchland">Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a>) argues that the concept of belief is like obsolete theories of times past such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_four_humours" title="The four humours">the four humours</a> theory of medicine, or the <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a> of combustion. In these cases science has not provided us with a more detailed account of these theories, but completely rejected them as valid scientific concepts to be replaced by entirely different accounts. The Churchland argue that our common-sense concept of belief is similar in that as we discover more about neuroscience and the brain, the inevitable conclusion will be to reject the belief hypothesis in its entirety.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is entirely wrong; however, treating people, animals, and even computers as if they had beliefs is often a successful strategy – The major proponents of this view, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lynne_Rudder_Baker" title="Lynne Rudder Baker">Lynne Rudder Baker</a>, are both eliminativists in that they hold that beliefs are not a scientifically valid concept, but they do not go as far as rejecting the concept of belief as a predictive device. Dennett gives the example of playing a computer at chess. While few people would agree that the computer held beliefs, treating the computer as if it did (e.g. that the computer believes that taking the opposition's queen will give it a considerable advantage) is likely to be a successful and predictive strategy. In this understanding of belief, named by Dennett <i>the <a href="/wiki/Intentional_stance" title="Intentional stance">intentional stance</a></i>, belief-based explanations of mind and behaviour are at a different level of explanation and are not reducible to those based on fundamental neuroscience, although both may be explanatory at their own level.</li></ul>
<p>Strategic approaches make a distinction between rules, norms and beliefs as follows:
</p>
<ul><li>Rules. Explicit regulative processes such as policies, laws, inspection routines, or incentives. Rules function as a coercive regulator of behavior and are dependent upon the imposing entity's ability to enforce them.</li>
<li>Norms. Regulative mechanisms accepted by the social collective. Norms are enforced by normative mechanisms within the organization and are not strictly dependent upon law or regulation.</li>
<li>Beliefs. The collective perception of fundamental truths governing behavior. The adherence to accepted and shared beliefs by members of a social system will likely persist and be difficult to change over time. Strong beliefs about determinant factors (i.e., security, survival, or honor) are likely to cause a social entity or group to accept rules and norms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief_formation_and_revision">Belief formation and revision</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Belief formation and revision">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Belief_revision" title="Belief revision">Belief revision</a></div>
<p>An extensive amount of scientific research and philosophical discussion exists around the modification of beliefs, which is commonly referred to as belief revision. Generally speaking, the process of belief revision entails the believer weighing the set of truths and/or evidence, and the dominance of a set of truths or evidence on an alternative to a held belief can lead to revision. One process of belief revision is <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian updating</a> and is often referenced for its mathematical basis and conceptual simplicity. However, such a process may not be representative for individuals whose beliefs are not easily characterized as probabilistic.
</p><p>There are several techniques for individuals or groups to change the beliefs of others; these methods generally fall under the umbrella of <a href="/wiki/Persuasion" title="Persuasion">persuasion</a>. Persuasion can take on more specific forms such as <a href="/wiki/Consciousness_raising" title="Consciousness raising">consciousness raising</a> when considered in an activist or political context. Belief modification may also occur as a result of the experience of outcomes. Because <a href="/wiki/Goal" title="Goal">goals</a> are based, in part on beliefs, the success or failure at a particular goal may contribute to modification of beliefs that supported the original goal.
</p><p>Whether or not belief modification actually occurs is dependent not only on the extent of truths or evidence for the alternative belief, but also characteristics outside the specific truths or evidence. This includes, but is not limited to: the source characteristics of the message, such as <a href="/wiki/Source_credibility" title="Source credibility">credibility</a>; <a href="/wiki/Peer_pressure" title="Peer pressure">social pressures</a>; the anticipated consequences of a modification; or the ability of the individual or group to act on the modification. Therefore, individuals seeking to achieve belief modification in themselves or others need to consider all possible forms of resistance to belief revision.
</p><p>Glover maintains that any person can continue to hold any belief if they would really like to<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-54">[54]</a></sup> (for example, with help from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ad_hoc_hypotheses" title="Ad hoc hypotheses"><i>ad hoc</i> hypotheses</a>). One belief can be held fixed, and other beliefs will be altered around it. Glover warns that some beliefs may not be entirely <a href="/wiki/Explicit_memory" title="Explicit memory">explicitly</a> believed (for example, some people may not realize they have racist belief-systems adopted from their environment as a child). Glover believes that people tend to first realize that beliefs can change, and may be contingent on their upbringing, around age 12 or 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snipa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/240px-Snipa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/360px-Snipa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/480px-Snipa.JPG 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snipa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Philosopher Jonathan Glover warns that belief systems are like whole boats in the water; it is extremely difficult to alter them all at once (for example, it may be too stressful, or people may maintain their biases without realizing it).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-54">[54]</a></sup></div></div></div><p> Glover emphasizes that beliefs are difficult to change. He says that one may try to rebuild one's beliefs on more secure foundations (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axioms" title="Axioms">axioms</a>), like building a new house, but warns that this may not be possible. Glover offers the example of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, saying: "[Descartes] starts off with the characteristic beliefs of a 17th-century Frenchman; he then junks the lot, he rebuilds the system, and somehow it looks a lot like the beliefs of a 17th-century Frenchman." To Glover, belief systems are not like houses but are instead like boats. As Glover puts it: "Maybe the whole thing needs rebuilding, but inevitably at any point you have to keep enough of it intact to keep floating."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Models_of_belief_formation">Models of belief formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Models of belief formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ripples_of_influence.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/220px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/330px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/440px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ripples_of_influence.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>We are influenced by many factors that ripple through our minds as our beliefs form, evolve, and may eventually change</div></div></div>
<p>Psychologists study belief formation and the relationship between beliefs and actions. Three types of <i>models of belief formation</i> and change have been proposed: conditional inference process models, linear models and information processing models.
</p><p><b>Conditional inference process models</b> emphasize the role of inference for belief formation. When asked to estimate the likelihood that a statement is true, people allegedly search their memory for information that has implications for the validity of this statement. Once this information has been identified, they estimate the likelihood that the statement would be true if the information were true, and the likelihood that the statement would be true if the information were false. If their estimates for these two <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probabilities</a> differ, people average them, weighting each by the likelihood that the information is true and false. Thus, information bears directly on beliefs of another, related statement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._2005_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._2005-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike the previous model, <b>linear models</b> take into consideration the possibility of multiple factors influencing belief formation. Using regression procedures, these models predict belief formation on the basis of several different pieces of information, with weights assigned to each piece on the basis of their relative importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._2005_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._2005-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Information processing models</b> address the fact that the responses people have to belief-relevant information is unlikely to be predicted from the objective basis of the information that they can recall at the time their beliefs are reported. Instead, these responses reflect the number and meaning of the thoughts that people have about the message at the time that they encounter it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._2005_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._2005-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some influences on people's belief formation include:
</p>
<ul><li>Internalization of beliefs during childhood, which can form and shape humans' beliefs in different domains. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> is often quoted as having said that "Common sense is the collection of prejudices acquired by age eighteen." Political beliefs depend most strongly on the political beliefs most common in the community where one lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Most individuals believe the <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> they were taught in childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charismatic_leader" title="Charismatic leader">Charismatic leaders</a> can form or modify beliefs (even if those beliefs fly in the face of all previous beliefs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Rational individuals need to reconcile their direct reality with any said belief; therefore, if belief is not present or possible, it reflects the fact that contradictions were necessarily overcome using <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_dissonance" title="Cognitive dissonance">cognitive dissonance</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">Advertising</a> can form or change beliefs through repetition, shock, or association with images of sex, love, beauty, and other strong positive emotions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Contrary to <a href="/wiki/Intuition" title="Intuition">intuition</a>, a delay, known as the <a href="/wiki/Sleeper_effect" title="Sleeper effect">sleeper effect</a>, instead of immediate succession may increase an advertisement's ability to persuade viewer's beliefs if a discounting cue is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li>Physical trauma, especially to the head, can radically alter a person's beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>However, even educated people, well aware of the process by which beliefs form, still strongly cling to their beliefs, and act on those beliefs even against their own self-interest. In her book <i>Leadership Therapy</i>, Anna Rowley states: "You want your beliefs to change. It's proof that you are keeping your eyes open, living fully, and welcoming everything that the world and people around you can teach you." This view implies that peoples' beliefs may evolve as they gain new experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prediction">Prediction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Prediction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Different psychological models have tried to predict people's beliefs and some of them try to estimate the exact probabilities of beliefs. For example, <a href="/wiki/Robert_S._Wyer" title="Robert S. Wyer">Robert Wyer</a> developed a model of subjective probabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._1970_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._1970-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WyerGoldberg_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-WyerGoldberg-90">[90]</a></sup> When people rate the likelihood of a certain statement (e.g., "It will rain tomorrow"), this rating can be seen as a subjective probability value. The subjective probability model posits that these subjective probabilities follow the same rules as objective probabilities. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_total_probability" title="Law of total probability">law of total probability</a> might be applied to predict a subjective probability value. Wyer found that this model produces relatively accurate predictions for probabilities of single events and for changes in these probabilities, but that the probabilities of several beliefs linked by "and" or "or" do not follow the model as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wyer,_R._S._1970_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer,_R._S._1970-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WyerGoldberg_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-WyerGoldberg-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Delusion">Delusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Delusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/DSM-5" title="DSM-5">DSM-5</a>, delusions are defined as fixed false beliefs that are not changed even when confronted with conflicting evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Psychiatrist and historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G.E._Berrios" title="G.E. Berrios">G.E. Berrios</a> (1940- ) has challenged the view that delusions are genuine beliefs and instead labels them as "empty speech acts", where affected persons are motivated to express false or bizarre belief statements due to an underlying psychological disturbance. However, the majority of mental health professionals and researchers treat delusions as if they were genuine beliefs.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emotion_and_beliefs">Emotion and beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Emotion and beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2020</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Research has indicated that <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotion</a> and cognition act in conjunction to produce beliefs, and more specifically emotion plays a vital role in the formation and maintenance of beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">

<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">

Durkheim's discussion of collective belief, though suggestive, is relatively obscure.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Companion+to+Epistemology&amp;rft.series=Just+the+Facts101&amp;rft.edition=2&amp;rft.pub=Content+Technologies+Inc.&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=9781478400028&amp;rft.aulast=Dancy&amp;rft.aufirst=Jonathan&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DWToMZZoTu_QC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABelief"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> (magazine), 11 June 2011 <a class="external text" href="https://www.newscientist.com/article/mg21028160.200-a-field-guide-to-bullshit.html" rel="nofollow">A field guide to bullshit | New Scientist</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150616042330/http://www.newscientist.com/article/mg21028160.200-a-field-guide-to-bullshit.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 16 June 2015 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> - "Intellectual black holes are belief systems that draw people in and hold them captive so they become willing slaves of claptrap. <li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Note for example the concept of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_Christian" title="Cultural Christian">cultural Christian</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-larson-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-larson_70-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFLarsonSusan_S._LarsonHarold_G._Koenig2000">Larson, David B.; Susan S. <li id="cite_note-russell-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-russell_72-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFRussell1927"><a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Russell, Bertrand</a> (1927). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20061119081311/http://www.positiveatheism.org/hist/russell0.htm" rel="nofollow">"Why I am Not a Christian"</a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.positiveatheism.org/hist/russell0.htm" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 19 November 2006<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">19 May</span> 2006</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Why+I+am+Not+a+Christian&amp;rft.date=1927&amp;rft.aulast=Russell&amp;rft.aufirst=Bertrand&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.positiveatheism.org%2Fhist%2Frussell0.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABelief"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> believe that women are inferior to men. Some <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> share this belief. At the time of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> of 1861–1865, many Southerners used passages from the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> to justify race-based <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>. Certain campaigners have used the Christian religion as a reason to persecute and to deny the rights of homosexuals, on the basis that the Christian biblical God disapproves of homosexuality, and by implication of homosexuals. Compare <a class="external free" href="http://www.godhatesfags.com" rel="nofollow">http://www.godhatesfags.com</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210307223124/https://www.godhatesfags.com/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 7 March 2021 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-:0-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:0_74-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:0_74-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Beauchamp, Philip (pseudonym of Jeremy Bentham) <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=qiRQAAAAIAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow"><i>Analysis of the Influence of Natural Religion on the Temporal Happiness of Mankind</i></a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200204213852/https://books.google.com/books?id=qiRQAAAAIAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 4 February 2020 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, 1822, R. Carlile, London, at page 76: "Of all human antipathies, that which the believer in a God bears to the unbeliever is the fullest, the most unqualified, and the most universal"</span>
</li>
